South Blvd. Video & News v. Charlotte Zoning Bd. of Adjustment


NO. COA97-824


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


129 N.C. App. 282; 498 S.E.2d 623; 1998 N.C. App. LEXIS 513


February 18, 1998, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
April 21, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 27, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by petitioner from judgment, order, and permanent injunction entered 16 December 1996 and order of contempt entered 24 January 1997 by Judge Julia V. Jones in Mecklenburg County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner adult bookstore owner (owner) appealed the order from the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina), which affirmed the decision of respondent City of Charlotte Zoning Board of Adjustment (board) ordering the owner to cease operation of an adult bookstore and adult mini-motion picture theater. The owner argued that the decision and order were based on an unconstitutionally vague interpretation of the term "preponderance."


OVERVIEW: The owner obtained a permit to conduct a business for the intended use of "video booths and retail sales," as long as the "preponderance" of the sales were non-adult in nature. Thereafter, a zoning inspector sent the owner a notice of violation of Charlotte, N.C., Zoning Ordinance § 12.518, which required that an adult bookstore or adult mini motion picture establishment as defined by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-202.10 had to be separated by 1500 feet from any protected area. After determining that the owner operated an adult bookstore and mini motion picture establishment, the board filed a successful motion for a permanent injunction requiring the owner to comply with the ordinance. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that the trial court did not err in upholding the board's decision, in which the board determined that the definition of "preponderance" was satisfied if "adult magazines" were given a predominant emphasis by display or location in a store. The court held that the use of the word "preponderance" in North Carolina's definition of adult bookstores was not unconstitutionally vague because it gave a person a reasonable opportunity to know what was prohibited by the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order issuing a permanent injunction against the owner and affirming the board's decision that the owner operated an adult business at its establishment.

State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co. v. Long


NO. COA97-801


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


129 N.C. App. 164; 497 S.E.2d 451; 1998 N.C. App. LEXIS 420


February 16, 1998, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
April 7, 1998, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 27, 1998.
Appeal dismissed by, in part State Farm Auto Ins. v. Comm'r, 349 N.C. 240, 558 S.E.2d 191, 1998 N.C. LEXIS 633 (1998)
Review denied by State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co. v. Long, 349 N.C. 240, 516 S.E.2d 608, 1998 N.C. LEXIS 632 (1998)
Affirmed by State Farm Mut. Auto. Ins. Co. v. Long, 350 N.C. 84, 511 S.E.2d 303, 1999 N.C. LEXIS 47 (1999)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by plaintiffs from order entered 17 April 1997 by Judge Narley L. Cashwell in Wake County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insurance companies sought review of the decision of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, the North Carolina Commissioner of Insurance and the North Carolina Secretary of Revenue (state), in an action by the insurance companies seeking a judgment that an insurance regulatory charge was a tax and that as a tax, it violated the federal and state constitutions.


OVERVIEW: The insurance companies brought an action against the state contending that an insurance regulatory charge was levied as a tax and not as a license or a user fee. They argued that once the charge was determined to be a tax, it violated the federal and state constitutions because it was a discriminatory tax with no legitimate purpose. The state countered that because the charge was neither levied nor collected as a contribution to the maintenance of the general government, the regulatory charge did not constitute a tax. The trial court granted the state's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the decision was affirmed. The court used a three-part test to determine whether the charge was a tax and concluded that the third prong regarding the revenue's ultimate use was dispositive. The court held that the fees, when collected, were placed in a segregated account, which were credited into an insurance fund and not to the general fund of the state in order to defray the cost of regulating the insurance industry. Because the ultimate use was regulation, it was not a tax. Also, the exclusion of the charge from the insurance companies' tax liability was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the state and the denial of the motion for summary judgment by the insurance companies. The court held that the insurance regulatory charge was not a tax.

State v. Taylor


NO. 97-136


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


128 N.C. App. 616; 495 S.E.2d 413; 1998 N.C. App. LEXIS 148



February 17, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from judgment entered 24 July 1996 by Judge William C. Griffin, Jr. in Martin County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No Error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of conviction from the Martin County Superior Court (North Carolina), which found defendant guilty of a violation of the county animal control ordinance. He contended the trial court erred when it denied his motion to dismiss the charge of violating the excessive noise by dogs offense.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's neighbor filed a complaint with a Martin County Animal Control Officer stating that the barking noises from defendant's dogs were keeping her up at night and disturbing her early in the morning. A jury found defendant guilty of violating the county animal control ordinance. Defendant appealed. The court affirmed the conviction. Defendant contended that the ordinance, as written, was unconstitutionally vague and indefinite in that it was difficult to determine what constituted excessive noise and when citizens were annoyed, disturbed, or frightened. The court held that Martin County's Animal Control Ordinance contained general terms in describing prohibited conduct. However, the terms also had commonly accepted meanings and were sufficiently certain to inform persons of ordinary intelligence as to what constituted a violation. In reviewing plenary evidence presented at trial of the habitual excessive noises caused by defendant's dogs and considering the common meanings of the words in the ordinance, the determination by the jury of a violation was not arbitrary or subjective.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for violating the county's animal control ordinance. The ordinance was not unconstitutional for vagueness or indefiniteness and the trial court was correct in denying defendant's motion to dismiss.

State v. Thompson


NO. COA97-236


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


128 N.C. App. 547; 496 S.E.2d 597; 1998 N.C. App. LEXIS 154



February 17, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from order entered 26 April 1996 by Judge Orlando F. Hudson, Jr., in Durham County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from an order of the Durham County Superior Court (North Carolina), reinstating charges of assault inflicting serious injury, assault on a female, and second degree trespass, which had been dismissed by the district court, and remanding to the district court for trial.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with assaulting two women, one of whom claimed to be a former domestic partner of defendant. Pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-534.1, setting forth the conditions of bail and pretrial release for crimes of domestic violence, defendant was denied pretrial release for nearly 48 hours. The district court granted defendant's motion to dismiss the charges and concluded that denial of pretrial release conditions amounted to punishment on the pending charges and that further prosecution would subject defendant to multiple punishments for the same offense. The State appealed. The superior court reinstated the charges and remanded to the district court for trial. The court affirmed and held that the detention was regulatory, not punitive, and did not constitute punishment for purposes of double jeopardy. The court found that the State had a legitimate interest in allowing judges experienced in criminal and domestic matters to determine the conditions of pretrial release for those who have been charged with domestic violence crimes, that pretrial detention of up to 48 hours was constitutionally permissible, and that § 15A-534.1 did not violate due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order reinstating the charges against defendant and remanded to the superior court for further remand to the district court.

United States Fid. & Guar. Co. v. Johnson


NO. COA97-292


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


128 N.C. App. 520; 495 S.E.2d 388; 1998 N.C. App. LEXIS 107


October 28, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
February 3, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by North Carolina Department of Transportation from order entered 20 November 1996 by Judge Donald R. Huffman in Moore County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sought review of an order of the Moore County Superior Court ( North Carolina) upon the motion of appellee, widow of a deceased employee, under . N.C. Gen. Stat. § 97-10.2(j) (1991). The superior court denied any recovery to employer on its lien for workers' compensation payments against insurance proceeds payable for the employee's wrongful death.


OVERVIEW: The employee was killed, and his passenger injured, in an auto accident while working for the employer, and his estate received an award of workers' compensation benefits. The corporate owner of the other vehicle had declared bankruptcy, and the widow divided the entire amount of available insurance proceeds with the passenger, and sought a determination of the employer's lien under § 97-10.2(j). The employee was 45 years old and supported the widow and two children. There was no other compensation for the death. The superior court provided the employer nothing on the lien. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the superior court was within its discretion where it could have reasonably concluded that such a result was equitable, because the funds available were insufficient to compensate for the decedent's death. The court held that § 97-10.2(j), allowing determination of the amount of the lien "if any" plainly provided the superior court with discretion to award nothing to the employer. The court found that a constitutional attack on § 97-10.2(j) was not raised in the superior court; thus, was waived.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court order, which denied any recovery to the employer on its lien for workers' compensation payments against insurance proceeds payable to the widow for an employee's wrongful death.

 

897 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Taylor


NO. COA96-1195


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


128 N.C. App. 394; 496 S.E.2d 811; 1998 N.C. App. LEXIS 26


June 4, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 20, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Mecklenburg County. 95 CRS 39363. Appeal by defendant from judgment entered 19 April 1996 by Judge John M. Gardner in Mecklenburg County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina), after he was convicted of second degree rape, challenging the trial court's use of his prior delinquency adjudication as an aggravating sentencing factor, the admission of his confession into evidence, and the juvenile court's decision to transfer his case to the superior court for trial.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found as an aggravating factor, under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 15A-1340.16(d)(18a) (Supp. 1996), that defendant had previously been adjudicated delinquent for second degree rape. Defendant argued that the trial court's use of § 15A-1340.16(d)(18a) violated constitutional ex post facto provisions because the law took effect after his first adjudication. The court disagreed because the new law did not retroactively punish conduct that was innocent when done nor aggravate the prior adjudication. There was no due process violation because the new law was in effect at the time of the second offense. The trial court's failure to invoke the judicial estoppel doctrine did not rise to the level of plain error where defendant failed to object. North Carolina law did not require that a juvenile be advised that he may be tried as an adult prior to interrogation. There was competent evidence to support the trial court's finding that defendant's confession was knowingly and voluntarily made. The court was bound by a prior decision from another panel rejecting the argument that the juvenile transfer statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-610(a) (1995), was unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence.

Liptrap v. City of High Point


NO. COA97-392


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


128 N.C. App. 353; 496 S.E.2d 817; 1998 N.C. App. LEXIS 38



January 20, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 97 CVS 10887. Appeal by plaintiffs from order entered 13 January 1997 by Judge Peter M. McHugh in Guilford County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employees sought review of the decision of the Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted a motion to dismiss pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6) (1990) on the ground that plaintiffs' action, which claimed defendant city's resolution freezing longevity pay was a breach of their employment contract, was barred by the two-year statute of limitations under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-53(1)(1996).


OVERVIEW: The employees filed an action claiming that the city's resolution freezing the amount of their longevity pay and subsequent refusals to pay additional amounts to those employees reaching greater increments of service constituted and continued to constitute breaches of their employment contracts. The trial court granted the city's motion to dismiss pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6) (1990) on the ground that the employees' action was barred by the two-year statute of limitations set forth in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-53(1)(1996). On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the employees' cause of action had accrued the day that city passed the resolution freezing the amount of longevity pay and breached their contracts with the employees. The court would not consider the subsequent refusals of the city to pay additional amounts to those employees reaching greater increments of service as a series of multiple breaches. The court concluded that the employees had failed to file suit within two years once the employees' cause of action had accrued, and their action was barred by the statute of limitations required by § 1-53(1).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of the city's motion to dismiss the employees' breach of employment contract claim because the employees' action had been time barred by the applicable statute of limitations.

Banks v. County of Buncombe


NO. COA97-180


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


128 N.C. App. 214; 494 S.E.2d 791; 1998 N.C. App. LEXIS 17


October 7, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 6, 1998, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiffs-appellants from judgment entered 3 September 1996 by Judge Ronald E. Bogle in Buncombe County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, students and board of education, challenged the Buncombe County Superior Court (North Carolina) judgment in favor of defendants, county and county board of commissioners, and intervenor board of education, in an action contesting the method of distributing funds to two school districts within the county. They asserted that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-430 applied and conflicted with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-472, which was repealed.


OVERVIEW: The students initiated an action against defendants challenging the method for distributing funds to city and county school districts. The board of education intervened. The trial court rendered judgment for defendants and intervenor and the students appealed. They asserted that the apportionment method of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-430 applied and that § 115C-430 conflicted with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-472(b)(2), which was repealed. The court held that the trial court properly found that § 105-472(b)(2) was not in conflict with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-430. N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-472(b)(2) dealt with the ad valorem method of distribution of the residual sales tax within a taxing district and did not supercede N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-472(b)(2), which dealt with distribution of residual sales tax dependent upon the levy of ad valorem taxes. Given the different subject matters, it did not appear that the legislature intended N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-430 to supersede N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-472(b)(2). N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-472 was not repealed. The specific statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-472(b)(2), governed distribution of the sales tax, not the general statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-430.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendants and intervenor in the students' action challenging the methods for distributing funds to school districts.

Virmani v. Presbyterian Health Servs. Corp. (In re Knight Publ'g Co.)


NO. COA96-1051


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


127 N.C. App. 629; 493 S.E.2d 310; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 1187; 26 Media L. Rep. 1257


May 19, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
November 18, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Upon writ of certiorari allowed through petition by Knight Publishing Company d/b/a The Charlotte Observer and John Hechinger from orders entered 24 January 1996 by Judge Marvin K. Gray, 9 February 1996 by Judge James U. Downs, 10 May 1996 by Judge Marcus L. Johnson, and 15 May 1996 and 22 May 1996 by Judge James U. Downs in Mecklenburg County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor publisher sought review of the decision of the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina), which denied its motions to intervene in an action between plaintiff doctor and defendant hospital to open the proceedings to the public and news media.


OVERVIEW: The court found that at common law, citizens had a right to inspect and copy public records, including judicial records, but the right was not absolute. The court found that access to public records was governed by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 132-1, which provided for liberal access. The court found that N.C. Const., art. I, § 18 required that the courts be an open forum. But the court recognized that in certain instances, the closure of proceedings would not violate this constitutional provision or even a First Amendment argument because freedom of speech was not absolute. The court held that the trial court was required to furnish the publisher a meaningful opportunity to be heard on its motion and to state reasons for its ruling supported by specific findings. The court also held that the publisher had the right to intervene under N.C. R. Civ. P. 24(b).


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court erred in denying the publisher's motions and acted contrary to N.C. Const., art. I, § 18 by not holding a hearing and without making findings of fact and conclusions of law. The matter was remanded with direction that the trial court unseal all documents previously sealed.

Maynor v. Onslow County


NO. COA96-1237


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


127 N.C. App. 102; 488 S.E.2d 289; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 785


May 20, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
August 5, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from order entered 2 May 1996 by Judge James R. Strickland in Onslow County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff manager brought an action against defendant county, seeking to prevent enforcement of Onslow County, N.C., Ordinance art. IV(c), which regulated adult businesses. The county filed a counterclaim, seeking an injunction enforcing the ordinance. The Onslow County Superior Court (North Carolina) granted summary judgment in favor of the county, and the manager appealed.


OVERVIEW: The manager sought prevention of enforcement of Onslow County, N.C., Ordinance art. IV(c), which provided that no adult business was permitted in any building located within 1000 feet of a building used as a dwelling. The adult business that she managed was within 1000 feet of a residence. The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment to the county. Although the record was unclear as to whether the manager had a proprietary interest in the business, the court treated the manager as if she was the appropriate real party in interest. The stated purpose of art. IV(c) was for promoting the health, safety, morals, and general welfare of the citizenry of the county, and it was well within the parameters of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 153A-121. It regulated the place and manner of expression only and was not violative of the First Amendment. Art. IV(c) was not unconstitutionally overbroad or vague. It was not intended to restrict any communication or protected speech or to deny adults access to the distributors of sexually oriented entertainment. Any element of vagueness did not affect the manager because she acknowledged that the ordinance applied to the business that she managed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the county and the denial of the manager's motion for summary judgment in the manager's action to prevent enforcement of an ordinance that regulated adult businesses.

Lewis v. City of Kinston


NO. COA96-1517


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


127 N.C. App. 150; 488 S.E.2d 274; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 791


June 5, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
August 5, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from order entered 4 October 1996 by Judge James D. Llewellyn in Lenoir County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employer sought review of a decision by the Lenoir County Superior Court (North Carolina), which declared unconstitutional a city ordinance that required appellee employee to be a resident of the county in order to retain his job with the employer.


OVERVIEW: The employee, who worked as a police officer for the employer, moved his residence to a neighboring county. The employer informed the employee that, pursuant to a city ordinance, Kingston City Code § 2-137 (1961), he was required to move back into the county or his employment would be terminated. The employee brought a declaratory judgment action testing the constitutionality of the ordinance. The parties filed cross-motions for summary judgment. The trial court granted the employee's motion, declared the ordinance unconstitutional, and enjoined its enforcement. The employer sought review and the court affirmed. The court found that the ordinance violated the Equal Protection Clause because it was not rationally related to a legitimate governmental purpose. The court noted that there were many legitimate reasons for residency requirements, including ensuring the emergency worker's close proximity to the city, his qualification to vote in local elections, and payment of taxes in the jurisdiction. None of those reasons applied in this case, the court found, because the ordinance required the employee to live in the surrounding county, not in the city in which he was employed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment that declared unconstitutional a city ordinance that required the employee to reside in the county in order to retain his job with the employer.

State v. Crabtree

OVERVIEW: Defendants were the owners of a beach bingo hall. Undercover investigators observed numerous violations of the state beach bingo laws at defendants' hall. 904 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Bethania Town Lot Comm. v. City of Winston-Salem


NO. COA96-1083


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


126 N.C. App. 783; 486 S.E.2d 729; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 622


May 13, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
July 15, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants from order entered 11 June 1996 by Judge L. Todd Burke in Forsyth County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of order entered by the Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina) permanently enjoining the city from annexing any part of appellee town.


OVERVIEW: The N.C. General Assembly ratified an act to revive the charter of the town. The town boundary, encompassing more than 2500 acres, was set out in a map more than 200 years earlier. The subject act delineated an area of approximately 400 acres as the corporate limits of the town. The city, by ordinance, subsequently attempted to annex property outside the corporate limits established by the act but within the 200-year-old limits. The trial court granted the town a permanent injunction prohibiting the city from annexing any portion of the alleged 2500 acres constituting the town and the city appealed. The town argued that the act was facially unconstitutional in violation of N.C. Const. art. II, § 24(1)(h) because it was a local act changing a township line. The court reversed, holding that the town was a town and not a township. A township was an involuntary corporation, such as a subdivision of a county, and not to a voluntary municipal corporation, such as a city or town. The legislature had full power to amend the charter of a municipal corporation, such as the town, at its pleasure, and such amendment took effect without any acceptance on the part of the town.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court and remanded the case with instructions to vacate the permanent injunction.

Bring v. North Carolina State Bar


NO. COA96-815


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


126 N.C. App. 655; 486 S.E.2d 236; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 598


April 29, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
July 1, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review granted by, Motion denied by Bring v. North Carolina State Bar, 347 N.C. 135, 492 S.E.2d 18, 1997 N.C. LEXIS 642 (1997)
Affirmed by Bring v. North Carolina State Bar, 348 N.C. 655, 501 S.E.2d 907, 1998 N.C. LEXIS 365 (1998)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by petitioner from order dated 31 May 1996 by Judge James C. Spencer, Jr. in Wake County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner law school graduate challenged a finding by the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) in favor of respondent state bar in an action filed by the graduate, contending that the authority vested in the Board of Law Examiners to determine who could be admitted to practice in North Carolina was an unconstitutional delegation of authority by the legislature.


OVERVIEW: A law school graduate was not allowed admission to practice law in North Carolina because she graduated from a law school that was not accredited by the American Bar Association (ABA). The graduate filed an action against the state bar alleging that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 84-24 was violative of N.C. Const. art. I, § 6 and art. II, § 1, as an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority because it allowed the Board of Law Examiners to make a determination regarding admission to the practice of law. The trial court found in favor of the state bar and the graduate appealed. The court found that § 84-24 was not an unconstitutional delegation of authority as long as the decision to limit admission was reasonably related to a rational objective and not, therefore, arbitrary. Limiting admission to graduates of ABA recognized schools was not arbitrary because it would be too cumbersome for the Board of Law Examiners to investigate each and every law degree granting institution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court in favor of the state bar.

In re Doe


NO. COA97-323


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


126 N.C. App. 401; 485 S.E.2d 354; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 372



June 3, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by petitioner from Order entered in closed session of Guilford County Superior Court (High Point Division) on 13 March 1997. Decided in the Court of Appeals 21 March 1997.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner minor appealed the Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina) order denying her petition for waiver of parental consent for minor's abortion upon an appeal for trial de novo. She asserted that the trial court erred in finding that she was not "well-informed enough" pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-21.8(e)(2) to make the abortion decision on her own.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-21.8, the minor sought judicial waiver of the parental consent requirement for minor's abortion, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-21.7. On appeal for trial de novo, the trial court denied her petition and she appealed asserting error in the finding that she was not "well informed enough" to make the abortion decision alone. The court treated the appeal as a petition for writ of certiorari because there was no right of direct appeal from the trial court order. On the merits it held that the petition was improperly denied. It found that the trial court's factual findings strongly supported an overall finding that the minor was mature and very informed regarding her decision to have an abortion. With no evidence to support its conclusion to the contrary, the trial court effectively substituted its own decision for that of the minor, in contravention of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-21.8(e)(1). The court also noted that the trial court failed to consider all three prongs of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-21.8(e). The failure of the trial court to determine whether the parental consent requirement should be waived pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-21.8(e)(2) was prejudicial error.


OUTCOME: The court treated the appeal as a petition for writ of certiorari. It reversed the order and ordered waiver of the parental consent requirement.

State v. Mason


NO. COA96-337


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


126 N.C. App. 318; 484 S.E.2d 818; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 345


January 9, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
May 20, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from judgment entered 30 November 1995 by Judge Donald W. Stephens in Wake County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No Error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted him of assault with a deadly weapon inflicting serious injury and voluntary manslaughter as a violent habitual offender and sentenced him to life imprisonment without parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that his indictment as a violent habitual felon should have been dismissed because of a fatal variance between the evidence presented and the indictment and the trial court erred in denying his motion to dismiss the charge of assault with a deadly weapon inflicting serious injury. The court found no error in the trial court's judgment. It found no merit in the argument that although the violent habitual offender indictment alleged that defendant committed the felony of assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill inflicting serious injury, he was actually convicted of the lesser included offense of assault with a deadly weapon inflicting serious injury. The court reasoned that a habitual offender indictment was not required to specifically refer to the predicate substantive felony and that assault with a deadly weapon inflicting serious injury was also a violent felony for which a defendant could be punished as a habitual offender. It found that the trial court properly denied defendant's motion to dismiss because the State presented more than a scintilla of evidence tending to show that defendant intended to seriously injure the victim.


OUTCOME: The court found no error in the trial court's judgment which convicted defendant of assault with a deadly weapon inflicting serious injury and voluntary manslaughter as a violent habitual offender and sentencing him to life imprisonment without parole.

State v. Woodberry


No. COA96-400


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


126 N.C. App. 78; 485 S.E.2d 59; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 367


January 28, 1997, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
April 15, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Publication Status of this Document has been Changed by the Court from Unpublished to Published 12 May 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by defendant from order entered 15 January 1996 by Judge Preston Cornelius in Alexander County Superior Court. From Alexander County. No. 92 CRS 2580-2582.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Alexander County Superior Court (North Carolina) imposing two consecutive sentences under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-31, malicious assault and battery in a secret manner with intent to kill, and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-32(a), assault with a deadly weapon with intent to kill inflicting serious injury. He contended that it violated the Double Jeopardy Clause of the U.S. Const. amend. V for it was one act.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the double jeopardy clause protected against multiple punishments for the same offense. In affirming the judgment of the trial court, the court noted that the United States Supreme Court had recognized that where Congress or a State legislature clearly and unambiguously expressed its intent to proscribe and punish exactly the same conduct under two separate statutes, the trial court in a single trial might have imposed consecutive sentences under the statutes. The question then was one of legislative intent. The court concluded that the general assembly intended that consecutive sentences could have been imposed against defendant who contemporaneously violated both N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-31 and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-32(a). While they contained three common elements, each contained specific additional elements not contained by the other. With each statute, the legislature clearly intended to provide a means of more severely punishing an offender whose conduct was egregious in a specific respect. Read together the plain language of the statutes indicated that consecutive sentences were permissible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

912 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


In re Appeal of Springmoor, Inc.


NO. COA96-113


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


125 N.C. App. 184; 479 S.E.2d 795; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 75


October 9, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 21, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by Springmoor, Inc., and Ammons, Inc., from order entered 16 November 1995 by the North Carolina Property Tax Commission.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers were denied applications for exemption by appellee county. The North Carolina Property Tax Commission held that the taxpayers were not entitled to an exemption because they did not meet the requirements of N.C. Gen. Stat. 105-275(32)(v). The taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers alleged § 105-275-(32)(v) discriminated against the class of homes for the aged, sick, or infirm, which were non-religious. The court reversed the judgment and held § 105-275(32)(v) unconstitutional as violative of the federal and state constitutions. For a challenged statute to pass the strict test of establishment clause neutrality, it had to have a secular legislative purpose, its principal or primary effect had to be one that neither advanced nor inhibited religion, and the statute had to not foster an excessive government entanglement with religion. The constitutional infirmity arose with § 105-275(32)(v) because it distinguished within the class of homes for the aged, sick, and infirm between those that were religiously affiliated and those that performed essentially the same functions but lacked any religious affiliation. Tax exempt status was granted to those with a religious affiliation and denied to those with no religious affiliation. There was no legitimate secular objective to justify the preference. Because the invalid portion of the statute could be severed from the valid portions of the statute, only § 105-275(32)(v) was deemed unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the tax commission denying the taxpayers tax-exempt status was reversed.

 

913 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Messer v. Town of Chapel Hill


NO. COA95-488


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


125 N.C. App. 57; 479 S.E.2d 221; 1997 N.C. App. LEXIS 1


February 1, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 7, 1997, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 22, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by plaintiffs from order entered 28 February 1995 by Judge Donald W. Stephens in Orange County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners appealed from an order of the Orange County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted defendant town's motion to dismiss the landowners' complaint for a declaration that the town's zoning ordinance was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The landowners filed an action to have the town's zoning ordinance declared invalid. The town filed a motion to dismiss, which the trial court granted. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the landowners' land-use challenges were not ripe for review. The landowners failed to allege that they filed a development plan or sought a variance in order to determine exactly how, or if, the zoning ordinance would affect their property. Thus, their "takings" claim under N.C. Const. art. I, § 19 was unripe. Also, the landowners' claim that the ordinance violated due process as an improper use of the police power was ripe for adjudication. A resolution of whether the ordinance went too far as an invalid exercise of the police power could not be measured until a final decision was made as to how the regulation would be applied to the landowners' property. Further, the landowners were unable to show that the zoning ordinance was arbitrary and capricious. Although they argued that the ordinance deprived them of the highest and best use of the property, such deprivation did not render the ordinance unconstitutional. Also, the ordinance had not yet been applied to the property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the landowner's complaint against the town that the zoning ordinance was unconstitutional.

 

914 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Guilford County Bd. of Comm'rs v. Trogdon


NO. COA95-499


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 741; 478 S.E.2d 643; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1288


February 20, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
December 17, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 18, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by defendants E. Wayne Trogdon and Peerless Insurance Company from judgment entered 10 February 1995 by Judge Judson D. DeRamus, Jr. in Guilford County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a superintendent, an insurer, and the new board of education, sought review of a judgment entered by the Guilford Count Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted appellees, a county, its board of commissioners, and taxpayers, summary judgment in their action that challenged the propriety of the superintendent's contract extension under the Merger Act.


OVERVIEW: The Merger Act governed the merger of several school systems. It provided that the new board of education would be solely responsible for personnel matters beginning with the 1993-94 school year. The procedures took effect when the voters approved them in a referendum election. One month prior to that date, the old board of education extended the superintendent's contract. The propriety of that action was challenged. The trial court entered summary judgment in favor of appellees. On appeal, that judgment was affirmed. Even though the old board of education had the power under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 115C-271 to extend the contract, the general assembly's approval of the Merger Act clearly stated its desire to divest that board of the power to make hiring decisions for the 1993-94 school year and beyond. Therefore, the Merger Act impliedly repealed the old board of education's power to handle such personnel matters. For that reason, the contract extension was improper. Moreover, the taxpayers had standing because the new board of education had stated that it would not take legal action to recover the monies paid to the superintendent pursuant to the extension.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's entry of summary judgment in favor of appellees in their action that challenged the propriety of a contract extension given to the superintendent under the Merger Act.

 

915 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Thomas v. North Carolina Dep't of Human Resources


NO. COA95-1310


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 698; 478 S.E.2d 816; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1291


August 27, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
December 17, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from order entered 4 October 1995 by Judge Preston Cornelius in Guilford County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an appeal from the Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina) plaintiff food stamp recipient argued that defendant North Carolina Department of Human Resources (NCDHR), refused to apply the court's ruling to other similarly situated plaintiffs; instead, the NCDHR continued to enforce its regulations interpreting the penalty provision of the Food Stamp Act, 7 U.S.C.S. § 2015(b)(1), in contravention of the opinion of the court.


OVERVIEW: The NCDHR argued that the appeal was moot because due to the USDA's change in regulations, the NCDHR was no longer in violation of the prior court ruling. The court disagreed, finding that an exception to the mootness doctrine applied in cases where a defendant voluntarily ceased its illegal conduct during the pendency of the appeal. The challenged conduct on the part of the NCDHR violated N. C. Const. art. I, § 6, the separation of powers doctrine, which required that the NCDHR and all other administrative agencies of the state give full effect to orders of the court and acquiesce in the statutory and constitutional interpretations determined by the court and the state Supreme Court. The order of the court determining that a regulation impermissibly conflicted with the enabling statute had the effect of invalidating or voiding the regulation, and no action whatsoever by the administrative agency could breath life into the invalidated regulation absent reversal or modification of the court's order by a higher court or absent legislative action sufficiently altering the enabling act.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded to the trial court.

 

916 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


City of Roanoke Rapids v. Peedin


NO. COA95-461


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 578; 478 S.E.2d 528; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1211


January 31, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
December 3, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiffs from order and judgment entered 14 February 1995 by Judge James E. Ragan in Halifax County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, municipalities and residents (residents), sought review of an order from the Halifax County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted summary judgment to defendants, county, members of the Halifax County Board of Health, and the Halifax County Health Director (board), in a declaratory judgment action regarding the validity of the board's enactment of smoking control rules.


OVERVIEW: The residents challenged the validity of the board's smoking rules, alleging that the board had exceeded its statutory authority and that the provisions of the smoking rules established discriminatory distinctions between similar businesses. The trial court granted summary judgment to the county and the board. On appeal, the court reversed and remanded for entry of summary judgment in the residents' favor. The court held that the board exceeded its rule-making powers when it improperly made distinctions based on policy concerns traditionally reserved for legislative bodies in its treatment of similar businesses in its requirements regarding whether a non-smoking area had to be provided and what percentage of the premises it had to cover. The court found that there were no health-related explanations for the disparate treatment, only concerns regarding economic hardship and difficulty of enforcement. The court also did not give effect to the severability clause of the smoking rules because it would be impossible to excise the invalid provisions while leaving the remainder of the rules intact.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting summary judgment to the board and remanded for entry of summary judgment in favor of the residents in the declaratory action regarding the board's smoking control rules.

State v. Johnson


NO. COA95-514


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 462; 478 S.E.2d 16; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1157


January 29, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
November 19, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied State v. Johnson, 345 N.C. 758, 485 S.E.2d 304, 1997 N.C. LEXIS 255 (1997)
Subsequent appeal at State v. Johnson, 2004 N.C. App. LEXIS 684 (N.C. Ct. App., May 4, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from judgment entered 20 October 1994 by Judge A. Leon Stanback in Durham County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error in the trial; order of forfeiture vacated.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Durham County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered a judgment convicting defendant of possession of cocaine and sentencing him to probation. The trial court also ordered defendant to pay restitution for the costs of analyzing the cocaine and to forfeit the money seized with the cocaine.


OVERVIEW: A police informant purchased cocaine from defendant's residence. Police officers executed a search warrant at defendant's residence. The officers found money and cocaine in defendant's pant pockets. Defendant was convicted of possession of cocaine and was placed on probation. The trial court ordered defendant to pay restitution for the cost of analyzing the cocaine and to forfeit the money found in his pant pockets. Defendant challenged the restitution and forfeiture order. On appeal, the court affirmed defendant's conviction and the restitution order, but vacated the order of forfeiture. Defendant was properly ordered to pay restitution pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90.95.3(b) because the statute allowed the trial court to impose a discrete cost for a specific purpose. The ordering of restitution for the analysis of drugs is clearly incidental to the primary function of the trial court. The money in defendant's pant pockets was not subject to forfeiture under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-112(a)(2) because there was no evidence linking the money to any selling or delivering of cocaine.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction and the order of restitution. The court vacated the order of forfeiture.

State v. Green


NO. COA95-936


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 269; 477 S.E.2d 182; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1055


May 14, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
November 5, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Review denied by, Motion denied by State v. Green, 345 N.C. 644, 483 S.E.2d 714, 1997 N.C. LEXIS 131 (1997)
Affirmed by State v. Green, 348 N.C. 588, 502 S.E.2d 819, 1998 N.C. LEXIS 364 (1998)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from judgments and commitments entered 26 January 1995 by Judge Narley L. Cashwell in Wake County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the orders of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) which convicted and sentenced him to mandatory life imprisonment for first degree sexual offense, 6 years imprisonment for attempted first-degree rape to run concurrently with the life sentence, and 15 years imprisonment for first-degree burglary to run consecutively after the life sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was 13 years old, confessed to breaking into the victim's apartment and forcing her into sexual acts. Defendant was tried as an adult, which he argued was an abuse of discretion. The court affirmed, finding that defendant cited no authority that a juvenile court had to consider his rehabilitative potential before transferring the case to adult court, but even if he did, the juvenile court did consider his history of assaultive behavior, the fact that the victim was a stranger, the strength of the probable cause evidence, and unadjudicated acts he admitted to a psychologist. Defendant's motion to suppress his confession was overruled. His mother was present when he admitted to breaking into the victim's residence. The court held that defendant's due process challenge to the constitutionality of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-610 was without merit. The court held that the imposition of the mandatory sentence of life imprisonment on defendant for first-degree sexual offense was neither cruel nor unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the transfer to adult court, the conviction, and sentence, holding that defendant had a fair trial free of prejudicial error.

Miracle v. North Carolina Local Gov't Emples. Retirement Sys.


NO. COA94-1241


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 285; 477 S.E.2d 204; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1050



November 5, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from judgment entered 17 June 1994 by Judge D. Jack Hooks, Jr., in Onslow County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee challenged a decision from the Onslow County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted summary judgment in favor of defendants, retirement system, its management, and the state, in the employee's action that challenged the constitutionality of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 128-24(5)(c) and alleged equitable estoppel.


OVERVIEW: The employee retired and took a job that allowed him to continue receiving his retirement benefits. After he retired, § 128-24(5)(c) was passed, which subjected employees that had already retired to a salary cap. In reviewing the trial court's decision to grant summary judgment in favor of defendants, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court found that the employee's retirement was deferred compensation to which he was contractually entitled. The court determined that § 128-24(5)(c) impaired this contractual obligation and that there was a genuine issue of material fact as to whether § 128-24(5)(c) was reasonable and necessary to serve an important public purpose. In order to establish a due process violation, the employee had the burden of showing that the retroactive application of § 128-24(5)(c) was arbitrary and irrational. The court determined that the record was not sufficiently developed on the employee's Fourteenth Amendment and N.C. Const. art. I, § 19 claims. Finally, the court noted that the employee had not established that the doctrine of equitable estoppel applied because there was no evidence of a false representation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment granted in favor of defendants on the employee's constitutional claims. The court affirmed the summary judgment granted in favor of defendants on the employee's equitable estoppel claim.

Barber v. Continental Grain Co.


NO. COA95-36


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 310; 477 S.E.2d 77; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1049



November 5, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiffs from orders entered 20 May 1994 and 6 June 1994 by Judge William C. Griffin, Jr., in Martin County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff farmers filed a complaint in Martin County Superior Court, North Carolina, against defendants, a fertilizer importer and a fertilizer maker, alleging breach of implied warranty of merchantability, fraud, unfair and deceptive trade practices, and negligence. The trial court entered summary judgment in favor of the importer and maker. The farmers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The farmers requested testing of their commercial fertilizer as authorized by the North Carolina Fertilizer Law pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 106-658 (1995) and N.C. Gen. Stat. § 106-662 (1995). A fertilizer inspector took only "unofficial" samples and reported the findings of nitrogen deficiency to the farmers. The court found that an applicable regulation required that an official sample had to come from a reliable source -- directly from containers located on the maker's or distributor's premises, from vehicles used to ship fluid fertilizer, or from vehicles owned by the distributor that were en route to a consumer. The court found that the sample came from the farmers' property and did not comply with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 106-662(b). The court held that the sample was properly disqualified as evidence and that the farmers had not fulfilled the testing prerequisite for suit set out in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 106-662(e)(4). The court found that the farmers' failed to carry their burden because an essential element of their claim was nonexistent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's entry of summary judgment in favor of the fertilizer importer and maker.

Fisher v. Fisher


NO. COA96-17


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 442; 477 S.E.2d 251; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1069


September 18, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
November 5, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected April 25, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by plaintiffs from order entered 6 November 1995 in Cabarrus County District Court by Judge Clarence E. Horton, Jr.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff grandparents sought review of an order by the Cabarrus County District Court (North Carolina), which dismissed their complaint against defendant mother seeking visitation with their grandchildren. The grandparents argued that only parents who resided with their children in an intact nuclear family were insulated from grandparent visitation actions pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 50-13.1(a) (1995).


OVERVIEW: The mother was the sole custodian of her two children. The grandparents filed a complaint against the mother seeking visitation with the children. The trial court dismissed the complaint, ruling that the grandparents lacked standing, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 50-13.1 (1995), to bring such a complaint. The grandparents appealed, arguing that it was only parents who lived with their children in an intact nuclear family that were protected from grandparent visitation actions pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 50-13.1(a) (1995). The grandparents further argued that because there was an ongoing custody dispute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 50-13.2(b)(1) allowed for visitation. The court affirmed, holding that a single parent living with her children was an intact family. Thus, the grandparents lacked standing to commence an action for visitation of their grandchildren pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 50-13.1(a) (1995). The court further held that although N.C. Gen. Stat. § 50-13.2 (b)(1) allowed grandparents standing to seek grandparent visitation when custody of a minor child was at issue, there was no evidence that the mother was involved in such a custody dispute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court dismissing the grandparents' complaint against the mother seeking visitation with their grandchildren.

Moore v. Evans


NO. COA95-862


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


124 N.C. App. 35; 476 S.E.2d 415; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 1014


April 18, 1996, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
October 15, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Pitt County. No. 94CVS927. Appeal by defendants T. H. Evans and Roger Paul from order entered 7 June 1995 by Judge David Q. LaBarre in Pitt County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant police officers appealed from the order of the Pitt County Superior Court (North Carolina), which denied their motion for summary judgment in plaintiff citizen's action alleging violations of his constitutional rights stemming from his arrest and incarceration for burglary.


OVERVIEW: The police arrested the citizen on the basis of the color of his clothing and his proximity to the burglary. He was jailed on a bond that was much higher than usual for the circumstances, and held in jail after another suspect confessed to the crime. The court held that the city had waived its governmental immunity by purchasing liability insurance, and thus summary judgment was properly denied on the citizen's state claims. The court also found issues of fact as to whether the arresting officer had probable cause to arrest the citizen. The court noted in particular the conflict between the officer's assertion that a positive identification had been made and the statements of the eyewitness that she had told the officer that she had not seen the suspect's face and could not identify him beyond the fact that the suspect was a black man in white clothing. The citizen's claim under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 against the chief of police could not be maintained, as he was acting in his official capacity and had governmental immunity. However, there was an issue of fact as to whether the arresting officer acted with reckless indifference to the citizen's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the police officers' motion for summary judgment regarding the state claims, and as to the federal constitutional claims against the arresting officer in his individual capacity. The court reversed the trial court's denial of the police officers' motion for summary judgment regarding the federal constitutional claims against the officers in their official capacities.

Town of Spruce Pine v. Avery County


No. COA95-639


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


123 N.C. App. 704; 475 S.E.2d 233; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 937


February 28, 1996, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
September 17, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants from judgment entered 23 June 1994 by Judge Lacy H. Thornburg in Avery County Superior Court and from judgment entered February 1, 1995 by Judge Melzer A. Morgan, Jr. in Caldwell County Superior Court. Avery County No. 94 CVS 193. Caldwell County No. 94 CVS 1257.



DISPOSITION: The order granting summary judgment to the State agencies on the County's cross-claim is reversed and the case is remanded to the trial court for entry of summary judgment for the County on its cross-claim against the State agencies for a declaratory judgment that the WSWPA is unconstitutional.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Counter claimant county sought review of a judgment of the Caldwell County Superior Court (North Carolina) that entered summary judgment in favor of counter defendant state agencies and held that the county did not have standing to bring the counterclaim against the agencies, which asserted that the amendments to the Water Supply Watershed Protection Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 143-214.5 and 143-214.6, were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The town brought an action against state agencies and county after the county and the state agencies refused to grant the town a building permit to construct a water supply intake. The county also filed a counterclaim against state agencies, asserting that the amendments to the Water Supply Watershed Protection Act (Act), N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 143-214.5 and 143-214.6, were unconstitutional. The trial court granted the state agencies' motion for summary judgment. On appeal, the court reversed, holding that pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 1-254, 1-265, and 160A-11, the county had standing to bring the declaratory judgment action. The county was entitled to challenge the constitutionality of the Act. The court held that although the procedural safeguards were adequate, the Act lacked meaningful guiding standards. Absent such standards, the applicable procedural safeguards alone were not enough to convert the Act into a lawful delegation of legislative power. Thus, the Act was an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power in violation of N.C. Const. art. I, § 6 and art. II, § 1. The case was remanded to the trial court for entry of summary judgment for the county.


OUTCOME: The court reversed a judgment of the trial court that entered summary judgment in favor of the state agencies and held that the county did not have standing to bring the counterclaim against town, which asserted that the amendments to the Water Supply Watershed Protection Act were unconstitutional.

Kaplan v. Prolife Action League


No. COA95-1065


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


123 N.C. App. 720; 475 S.E.2d 247; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 942


May 21, 1996, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
September 17, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiffs from order entered May 15, 1995 by Judge Thomas W. Ross in Guilford County Superior Court. NO. 92 CVS 3228.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina) granted partial summary judgment to defendants, a pro-life organization and picketers, on the basis that plaintiffs, a physician and his family, failed to show violations of the North Carolina Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act (NC RICO), N.C. Gen. Stat. § 75D-1 et seq. The physician and his family appealed.


OVERVIEW: The picketers protested abortion outside the personal residence of the physician and his family. The trial court found that the physician and his family did not proffer evidence of "pecuniary gain" by the picketers or the organization, as required by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 75D-2(c) (1990). On appeal, the court affirmed and noted that the statute limited itself specifically to organized unlawful activity, the purpose or effect of which was to derive pecuniary gain. The court noted that there was no evidence that the picketers incurred gain or that the gain incurred by the pro-life organization in the form of contributions was connected with the picketing activity at issue. Alternatively, the court affirmed the judgment on the basis that the physician and his family failed to demonstrate any injury or damage to property cognizable under the NC RICO. Specifically, a loss of the use and enjoyment of one's personal residence did not constitute an injury to property recoverable under federal Racketeer Influenced and Corrupt Organizations Act, 18 U.S.C.S. § 1961 et seq., and the general assembly did not intend to provide the NC RICO with a broader remedial stroke than its federal counterpart.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which granted partial summary judgment to the pro-life organization and picketers in the action of the physician and his family for violations of the NC RICO.

Martin v. Continental Ins. Co.


No. COA95-633


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


123 N.C. App. 650; 474 S.E.2d 146; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 860


February 28, 1996, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
September 3, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Lenoir County No. 91 CVS 1254. Appeal by plaintiffs from summary judgment entered 4 January 1993 by Judge W. Russell Duke, Jr., in Lenoir County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff driver, who was operating a fleet vehicle, recovered a judgment against defendant motorist and sought underinsured motorist coverage from defendant fleet insurer. In a declaratory judgment action the Lenoir County Superior Court (North Carolina) granted the insurer's motion for summary judgment, holding that coverage was not available under the fleet policy, and the driver appealed.


OVERVIEW: The driver of a fleet vehicle was injured in an accident and recovered a judgment in excess of the coverage available under the other motorist's policy and the underinsured motorist (UIM) limit of her personal policy. The fleet insurer claimed that it was not liable because UIM coverage had been rejected under a fleet policy issued in Kansas. Although UIM coverage was not rejected on the form mandated by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-279.21(b)(4) (1988), the insurer argued that Kansas law governed because the application of North Carolina law would violate due process. The court held that under the close connections rule the fleet policy was governed by North Carolina law and the rejection of UIM coverage was ineffective. Summary judgment was erroneously granted because it conflicted with the public policy enunciated in N.C. Gen. Stat. § 58-3-1, which stated that all insurance policies on property in the state were deemed to have been made within the state. Section 20-279.21(b)(4) did not extend the Rate Bureau's jurisdiction but merely provided a standardized form for the rejection of UIM coverage.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment to the insurer.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher appealed a decision of the Caldwell County Superior Court (North Carolina), which affirmed the teacher's dismissal as a career teacher by respondent Caldwell County Board of Education (board).


OVERVIEW: The teacher approached the entrance of a pool room one afternoon armed with a shot gun, fully loaded with a shell in the chamber, and he also carried a loaded pistol partially covered by his jacket. State v. Ballenger


No. COA95-847


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


123 N.C. App. 179; 472 S.E.2d 572; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 685


April 15, 1996, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
July 16, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Guilford County. No. 94 CRS 63378, 94 CRS 63379. Appeal by the State from order entered 5 May 1995 by Judge Catherine C. Eagles in Guilford County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff state appealed an order of the Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina), which dismissed criminal charges against defendant drug dealer for violation of the North Carolina Controlled Substances Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 90-86 et seq. (1993).


OVERVIEW: The drug dealer was arrested and charged with felonious possession of marijuana, and possession with intent to sell or deliver marijuana, in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-95(a). Pursuant to the North Carolina Controlled Substance Tax, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 105-113.105 et seq. (1992), the Department of Revenue issued a tax assessment against the drug dealer who paid the tax. The drug dealer then filed a motion to dismiss the criminal charges, alleging that a criminal prosecution would violate the prohibition against successive punishments for the same offense contained in the Double Jeopardy Clause of U.S. Const. amend. V and guaranteed under the "law of the land" clause of N.C. Const. art. I, § 19. The trial court granted the drug dealer's motion, and the state appealed. The court held that the North Carolina Controlled Substance Tax did not have such fundamentally punitive characteristics as to render it violative of the prohibition against multiple punishments for the same offense contained in the Double Jeopardy Clause. Therefore, the trial court erred in concluding that prosecution of the drug dealer on the drug possession charges would subject him to double jeopardy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the criminal charges against the drug dealer and remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

929 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Houston


No. COA95-1012


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


122 N.C. App. 648; 471 S.E.2d 127; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 480


April 22, 1996, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
June 4, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wake County. Nos. 94 CR 23257, 94 CR 23258. Appeal by defendant from judgment entered 13 January 1995 by Judge Ronald Stephens in Wake County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted him of two counts of willful failure to file state income taxes in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-236(9).


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant contended that the state income tax, as applied to him, was unconstitutional because N.C. Const. art. I, § 1 allowed a person to enjoy the fruits of their labor. The court declined to rule on that issue because defendant had not raised it at the trial level. The court instead reviewed the record to determine whether the evidence was sufficient to have warranted submission of the charges to the jury. Defendant contended that the element of willfulness was negated by his sincere belief that the State was powerless to impose income taxes upon him under N.C. Const. art. I, § 1, thereby rendering his case subject to dismissal before being sent to the jury. The court disagreed, holding that while a good-faith misunderstanding of the law regarding state income taxes could have negated willfulness, a good-faith disagreement with the law did not. Because there was conflicting evidence as to defendant's willfulness, the issue was properly submitted to the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

State v. Hines


No. COA95-733


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


122 N.C. App. 545; 471 S.E.2d 109; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 474


March 19, 1996, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
June 4, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Hertford County. No. 94 CRS 41. Appeal by defendant from judgment entered 5 January 1995 by Judge W. Russell Duke, Jr. in Hertford County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Hertford County Superior Court (North Carolina), which denied defendant's motion to set aside the verdict, dismiss the charges, or grant a new trial, convicted defendant upon the jury verdict for willfully intimidating or attempting to intimidate an election officer in the discharge of his duties and for willfully communicating threats, and ordered defendant to pay a fine and court costs.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with willfully intimidating or attempting to intimidate an election officer in the discharge of his duties and for willfully communicating threats. The jury entered a guilty verdict against defendant on both of the charged offenses. The offenses were consolidated for judgment. The trial court denied defendant's motion to set aside the verdict, dismiss the charges, or grant a new trial, convicted defendant upon the jury verdict, suspended defendant's 30 day sentence, and ordered defendant to pay a fine and court costs. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held (1) that under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-275(11), an "election officer" was anyone charged with an election duty and members of county boards of elections were "election officers" for the purpose of applying § 163-275(11); and (2) that the trial court did not err in denying defendant's motion for appropriate relief, as there was no lesser included offense that applied to defendant's actions and § 163-275(11) was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant of willfully intimidating or attempting to intimidate an election officer in the discharge of his duties and of willfully communicating threats, the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to set aside the verdict, dismiss the charges, or grant a new trial, and the trial court's order to defendant to pay a fine and court costs.

State v. Ellison


No. COA96-2


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


122 N.C. App. 638; 471 S.E.2d 130; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 478


May 27, 1996, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
June 4, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Cabarrus County. No. 95 CRS 15045. Appeal by defendant from judgments entered 20 July 1995 by Judge Catherine C. Eagles in Cabarrus County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment from the Cabarrus County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted him of operating a vehicle without having a driver's license.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for driving a 1983 Plymouth automobile without a license. Defendant contended that the Uniform Driver's License Act either was unconstitutionally applied to his case or, in the alternative, that he qualified under an exemption from licensing. The court disagreed. First, the fact that defendant had returned his license to the department of motor vehicles did not mean that he was free from statutory restrictions regarding licensing because the right to operate a motor vehicle upon the highways was a privilege that could have been exercised only in accordance with legislative restrictions. Second, the court held that there was no merit in defendant's argument that he was operating a "road machine" and not a motor vehicle, thereby exempting him from having to have a driver's license under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 20-8 (1993). Although the term "road machine" was not defined in § 20-8, it meant a vehicle, such a tractor, that involved only temporary operation for purposes other than travel. Defendant's automobile was not a "road machine," but a motor vehicle.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Mahoney v. Ronnie's Rd. Serv.


No. COA94-706


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


122 N.C. App. 150; 468 S.E.2d 279; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 221


March 22, 1995, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
April 2, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wake County No. 93 CVS 7637. Appeal by plaintiff from order entered 19 May 1994 by Judge Donald W. Stephens in Wake County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, husband and wife, sought review of a decision of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which rendered summary judgment in favor of defendants, manufacturer and parent corporation, in plaintiffs' action against the manufacturer, its parent corporation, and defendant mechanic for negligence, breach of warranty, strict liability, and absolute liability.


OVERVIEW: The husband was injured when a defective airbrake on the moving van he was driving in the course of his employment discharged as he was removing the covering of the airbrake. The husband had been advised to remove the covering by the mechanic in order to facilitate replacement. In the ensuing action for damages, the trial court rendered summary judgment in favor of defendants on the basis of the North Carolina's statute of repose under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-50(6). Plaintiffs argued that § 1-50(6) was unconstitutional and, in the alternative, that Arizona' statute of repose applied. The court affirmed the decision of the trial court and found that § 1-50(6) had long ago been ruled constitutional by the state supreme court. The court also found that the North Carolina statute of repose applied. Although plaintiffs were Arizona residents and the husband worked for the Arizona company that bought the truck with the defective brake, the brake was manufactured in North Carolina and the injury occurred in North Carolina. Thus, under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 25-1-105 (1995), North Carolina had the most significant relationship to the transaction and the parties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's grant of summary judgment in favor of the manufacturer and its parent company in plaintiffs' action for negligence, breach of warranty, strict liability, and absolute liability against defendants.

Miltland Raleigh-Durham v. Mudie


No. COA95-313


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


122 N.C. App. 168; 468 S.E.2d 275; 1996 N.C. App. LEXIS 223


January 22, 1996, Heard In The Court Of Appeals  
April 2, 1996, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Durham County No. 92 CVS 04677. Appeal by defendant from order entered 15 December 1994 by Judge D. Jack Hooks, Jr. in Durham County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party filed a lawsuit in the Durham County Superior Court (North Carolina), against defendant tortfeasor for fraud, breach of duty, and unfair and deceptive practices. Plaintiff attached defendant's property pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 1-440 et seq., defendant moved to dissolve the order of attachment on the grounds that it was unconstitutional, the motion was denied, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff filed an action against defendant but was unable to obtain service so plaintiff attached defendant's property on the basis that he was a non-resident. Defendant raised numerous assignments of error but the court only considered one because the others did not comply with N.C. R. App. P 10(c)(1) (1996). The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to dissolve the order of attachment. The court held that defendant's due process rights were not violated by the attachment without notice or a hearing under N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 1-440 et seq. because 1) defendant was not a resident of North Carolina when the attachment proceedings were commenced, 2) defendant refused to accept service several times at an address he had verified, and 3) defendant's evasion of the judicial system constituted an "exigent circumstance." The court rejected defendant's argument that his due process rights were violated by the fact that the damages at issue were unliquidated failed because the attachment order specified a sum certain.


OUTCOME: The order of the trial court, which denied defendant's motion to dissolve the order of attachment granted on behalf of plaintiff, was affirmed.
