Boesche v. Raleigh-Durham Airport Auth.


No. 9215SC23


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


111 N.C. App. 149; 432 S.E.2d 137; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 717; 8 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1132


December 9, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
July 20, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected August 19, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by plaintiff from order entered 4 November 1991 by Judge Robert H. Hobgood in Orange County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: The trial court's decision is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff employee appealed from an order of the Orange County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted defendant employers' motion to dismiss the employee's complaint alleging a wrongful discharge in violation of his rights under the Fourth Amendment for refusing to submit to a urine drug test.


OVERVIEW: The employee, an airport maintenance worker, filed a complaint alleging that he was discharged for refusing to submit to an unconstitutional drug test. He contended that the discharge violated his constitutional rights and his right under common law not to be discharged from employment in bad faith or for reasons contravening public policy. The employers filed a motion to dismiss the complaint on the grounds that it failed to state a claim upon which relief could be granted. The trial court granted the motion. The court affirmed, noting first that the employee was an employee-at-will and that he could not state a cause of action for wrongful discharge under the public policy exception to the at-will-employment doctrine unless the employers' drug testing program was unconstitutional. The court held that the program was not unconstitutional because the employee was in a position in which public safety or the safety of others was an overriding concern. The court found that, because the random drug testing procedure was constitutional, there was no violation of the employee's procedural or substantive due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order granting the employers' motion to dismiss.

 

974 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Murphy v. Glafenhein


No. 9224SC698


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


110 N.C. App. 830; 431 S.E.2d 241; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 666


May 27, 1993, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
July 6, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant Gene Manning d/b/a Manning, Tractor, Truck and Equipment from order filed 23 March 1992 in Yancey County Superior Court by Judge Beverly T. Beal.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant seller of farm equipment appealed from the order of the Yancey County Superior Court (North Carolina) denying its motions to dismiss plaintiff injury victim's complaint and the cross claims filed by defendant, executrix of purchaser's estate, on the grounds that the trial court lacked of personal jurisdiction.


OVERVIEW: The buyer purchased a trailer in Tennessee from the seller to haul a farm tractor from North Carolina to Tennessee. While driving through North Carolina, the buyer collided with the injury victim and was killed. The injury victim filed an action against the seller and the executrix who filed a cross-claims against the seller. The seller filed a motion to dismiss the claims pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1, N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(2), which was denied. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the trial court had personal jurisdiction over the seller under North Carolina's long-arm statute, N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-75.4(1)(d) (1983), because the seller's contacts with North Carolina were sufficient to meet the due process requirements associated with the exercise of personal jurisdiction. The seller had a business located near the North Carolina state line, which catered to North Carolina residents and resulted in sales and deliveries of trailers to North Carolina residents. Additionally, the seller had just become a dealer for a North Carolina company. Finally, North Carolina had an interest in adjudicating the dispute because it involved injuries to one of its residents.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying the seller's motion to dismiss on the basis that the trial court lacked personal jurisdiction over the seller.

 

975 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Fulton Corp. v. Justus


No. 9210SC15


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


110 N.C. App. 493; 430 S.E.2d 494; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 561


December 8, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 15, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from judgment entered 8 November 1991 by Judge Dexter Brooks in Wake County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) granted summary judgment to defendant, the Secretary of Revenue, in plaintiff corporation's suit that sought a refund of the amount paid in intangibles tax, a declaratory judgment that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-203 was unconstitutional, and attorney's fees. The corporation appealed.


OVERVIEW: The corporation held stock in six corporations. Of the six, only one conducted business in North Carolina. The corporation filed an intangible personal property tax return and brought suit challenging the constitutionality of the state's intangibles tax levied on ownership of corporate stock. The corporation argued that the taxing provision, § 105-203, violated the Commerce Clause of the United States Constitution by increasing the tax liability for shares of stock of corporations which have business activities, property locations, and tax liabilities outside of North Carolina and by lessening the tax liability for shares in corporations whose business and property were largely or completely in North Carolina. On appeal, the court held that (1) the corporation had standing to challenge the taxing scheme, (2) that the taxing scheme violated the Commerce Clause because the tax facially discriminated against interstate commerce in that shareholders of out-of-state corporations were required to pay intangibles taxes on a higher percentage of shares than shareholders of corporations operating solely in North Carolina, and (3) that the provisions of the taxing scheme were severable.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court was reversed, and the matter was remanded for entry of a judgment that the intangibles tax provision at issue was in violation of the Commerce Clause. The court excised the language of the statute that increased the tax liability for owners of stock in corporations whose business and property was not completely in North Carolina. The court further held that the corporation was not entitled to a refund.

Guilford County Bd. of Educ. v. Guilford County Bd. of Elections


No. 9218SC421


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


110 N.C. App. 506; 430 S.E.2d 681; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 575


March 31, 1993, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 15, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiffs from order and declaratory judgment entered 17 January 1992 by Judge Peter M. McHugh in Guilford County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, county board of education and individuals, appealed from an order and declaratory judgment entered in Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina), which ruled that 1991 N.C. Sess. Laws ch. 78 did not violate any of the provisions of the North Carolina Constitution. Plaintiffs filed suit against defendants, county board of elections, county board of commissioners, and city boards of education, seeking injunctive relief.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs asserted that because 1991 N.C. Sess. Laws ch. 78 (Act) affected only Guilford county, it was an unconstitutional local act. The court ruled that the Act was not violative of any of the provisions of the North Carolina Constitution and affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court determined that, in deciding to consolidate the school administrative units of Guilford County, the legislature made a rational distinction reasonably related to the Act's purpose of pursuing the goals of excellence and equity in educational opportunity for all children of Guilford County. Therefore, the Act withstood the reasonable classification analysis. Although the act singled out Guilford County for special benefits and added burdens, it did not violate the uniformity requirements of N.C. Const. art. IX, § 2(1). The court also held that the minimum funding provision of the Act did not violate N.C. Const. art. IX, § 2(2).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment which declared the legislative enactment to be constitutional and valid and denied plaintiffs' prayer for injunctive relief.

Hope v. Charlotte-Mecklenburg Bd. of Educ.


No. 9226SC206


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


110 N.C. App. 599; 430 S.E.2d 472; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 581


March 1, 1993, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 15, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by petitioner from judgment entered 31 October 1991 by Judge Marvin K. Gray in Mecklenburg County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teacher sought review of a judgment from the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina), which affirmed the decision of respondent board of education to dismiss the teacher from her employment.


OVERVIEW: The board of education dismissed the teacher from her employment. The trial court affirmed the decision of the board of education. On appeal, the teacher contended that she was denied due process in the hearing before the board of education because the lawyer who presented the case against her was a member of the same law firm as the lawyer who advised the board of education at the dismissal hearing. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that: (1) the teacher's argument that the conflicting roles played by the attorneys deprived her of an impartial decision maker and violated principles of fundamental fairness did not hold up because the board of education was the decision maker, not its attorney, who acted only in an advisory capacity; (2) the board of education was presumed to act correctly, and absent a showing of bias or unfair prejudice, the teacher could not prevail in her argument; (3) there was substantial evidence to support the teacher's dismissal on grounds of insubordination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which affirmed the board of education's dismissal of the teacher.

In re Appeal of Lee Memory Gardens


No. 9210PTC485


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


110 N.C. App. 541; 430 S.E.2d 451; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 577


April 15, 1993, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 15, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by taxpayer Lee Memory Gardens, Inc. from the final decision of the North Carolina Property Tax Commission entered 24 February 1992 by Vice-Chairman John A. Cocklereece.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged an order of the North Carolina Property Tax Commission determining that the undeveloped land set aside for burial purposes was not exempt from taxation by appellee county under N. C. Gen. Sat. § 105-278.2.The taxpayer argued that the Commission erred in its ruling, that the county assessor used an incorrect valuation method, and that the county unconstitutionally discriminated against the taxpayer in its valuation.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was a corporation that owned undeveloped, unmapped land as part of a tract of land set apart as a cemetery. The Commission's findings were conclusive when upon reviewing the entire record, they were supported by competent, substantial evidence. Under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-278.2(a), real property set apart for burial purposes was tax exempt unless it was owned and held for sale or rental. The taxpayer was not holding undeveloped land for his burial. Since the undeveloped land was irrevocably dedicated for exclusive use as a cemetery, the taxpayer could only hold it for the purpose of sale to others as burial sites. The taxpayer's land did not fall under the exemption of § 105-278.2(a) as tax-exempt property. The taxpayer could only challenge the appraisal method if it overcame the presumption that ad valorem property tax assessments were correct. The taxpayer did not carry its burden of showing this by competent and substantial evidence when it did not establish that the property assessment was substantially greater than the true value of the assessed property. There was no evidence of discrimination against the taxpayer in the valuation. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

In re Freeman


No. 9229SC50


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


109 N.C. App. 100; 426 S.E.2d 100; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 202


December 10, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
February 16, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by respondent from order entered 24 October 1991 in Rutherford County Superior Court by Judge C. Walter Allen.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant teacher sought review of an order of the Rutherford County Superior Court (North Carolina), which affirmed his dismissal as a career teacher by the appellee Rutherford County Board of Education (board).


OVERVIEW: The teacher was charged with felonies arising from possession and manufacture of marijuana. The criminal charges were subsequently dismissed. At a hearing before the board, police officers testified that they had found marijuana plants, marijuana, and other paraphernalia at or near the teacher's home, and that the teacher had told them he smoked marijuana. The teacher made no objection to the evidence. The board ordered the teacher's dismissal, and the teacher appealed. The teacher contended that the criminal proceedings against him had been dismissed because the search and seizure had violated his Fourth Amendment rights and that, therefore, the board should not have considered the evidence of the police officers. On appeal, the court found that the teacher was permitted to raise, for the first time in superior court, objections to evidence considered by an administrative agency, pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 150B-51(b)(5). However, the court rejected the teacher's argument that the evidence presented by the police officers should not have been considered because the court found no evidence in the record that revealed why the criminal proceedings had been dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order dismissing the teacher.

State v. Morgan


No. 9118SC830


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


108 N.C. App. 673; 425 S.E.2d 1; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 122


October 16, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 19, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by the State from Orders entered 16 November 1990 and 7 March 1991 by Judge Joseph E. Turner in Guilford County District Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed as to the entry of the Not Guilty verdict and Remanded for a new trial on the second DWI charge, and Affirmed as to the entry of the Prayer for Judgment Continued on the charge of DWLR.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of orders from Guilford County District Court (North Carolina), which set aside a guilty verdict and judgment for DWI entered against appellee driver, entered a not guilty verdict for that charge, set aside a guilty judgment for DWLR, and resentenced the defendant by entering a prayer for judgment continued on that charge.


OVERVIEW: The driver was arrested for DWI twice within two hours and charged with DWLR on the second arrest. After a trial, the lower court entered guilty verdicts and judgments and imposed sentences on all three charges. Two days later, the lower court set aside the verdict and judgment in the second DWI charge and entered a not guilty verdict for that charge, and the lower court set aside the judgment in the DWLR charge and resentenced the driver by entering a prayer for judgment continued on that charge. The state filed a motion to set aside the lower court's order, which the lower court denied. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court held that the lower court had authority to set aside the guilty verdict if it was contrary to the weight of the evidence and that the lower court's decision to do so was not an abuse of discretion. The court held that the lower court did not have authority to enter a not guilty verdict without a new trial. The court held that the lower court properly resentenced the driver for DWLR upon a determination that the original sentence was not supported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed as to the lower court's entry of the not guilty verdict for DWI, remanded for the state to prosecute a new trial on the driver's DWI charge, and affirmed as to the entry of the prayer for judgment continued on the charge of DWLR.

Nelson v. Battle Forest Friends Meeting


No. 9118SC670


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


108 N.C. App. 641; 425 S.E.2d 4; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 123


August 25, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 19, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants from judgment filed 24 January 1991 and amended 19 February 1991 in Guilford County Superior Court by Judge W. Steven Allen.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a church and a minister, challenged a judgment of the Guilford County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted a summary judgment in favor of plaintiff adjoining landowner. The landowner filed a complaint for trespass and to quiet title, alleging that defendants were trespassing on a contested strip of property. The trial court quieted title in the landowner and permanently enjoined defendants from trespassing on the property.


OVERVIEW: The disputed property was a narrow strip that lay between a road and abandoned railroad tracks. Both parties claimed ownership of the property vested in them by virtue of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-44.2(a) (Supp. 1992). The court reversed and remanded. The court held as follows: (1) the abandoned railroad easement and the road's right-of-way adjoined; (2) therefore, the exception in § 1-44.2(a), extending ownership to the nearest edge of the public road right-of-way, applied; (3) title to the disputed strip therefore was vested in the church as adjacent property owner; (4) summary judgment in favor of the landowner was error; and (5) the appeal of the temporary restraining order was dismissed because the order had expired prior to the appeal and was moot.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment in favor of the landowner and remanded the case for consideration of the question of the constitutionality of the statute vesting title of abandoned railroad right-of-ways in the adjacent landowners.

Woodard v. North Carolina Local Governmental Emples.' Retirement Sys.


No. 9110SC1024


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


108 N.C. App. 378; 424 S.E.2d 431; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 99


May 14, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 5, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants from orders denying defendants' motions to dismiss the complaint and from order certifying the action as a class action. These orders were entered 28 June 1991 in Wake County Superior Court by Judge Narley L. Cashwell.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed as to the trial court's denial of defendants' motion to dismiss on the issue of constitutional impairment of obligation of contract and as to the certification of the lawsuit as a class action. Reversed and remanded to the Superior Court with instructions to enter motions to dismiss on the issues of: Plaintiffs' 42 U.S.C. § 1983 action and plaintiffs' breach of fiduciary duty claim.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an action challenging an amendment to a retirement disability statute in place for local government employees in North Carolina, defendants appealed from orders of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) that denied their motions to dismiss the complaint and certified the action as a class action.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs challenged an amendment to a North Carolina retirement disability statute in place for local government employees. Plaintiffs alleged that, because their rights had vested under the previous statute, the amended statute violated their Due Process and Equal Protection rights actionable under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, that it constituted an unconstitutional impairment of the obligations of contracts in violation of U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, and that it constituted a breach of fiduciary duty. The trial court denied defendants' motions to dismiss the complaint and certified the action as a class action. The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint on the issue of constitutional impairment of obligation of contract, and as to the certification of a class action. The court reversed and remanded, however, the trial court's denial of defendants' motion to dismiss on the issues of plaintiffs' § 1983 action and breach of fiduciary duty claim. The court found that the statute of limitations had run on the § 1983 claim, and neither the equitable doctrine of demand and refusal, nor the continuing violation doctrine, saved this cause of action.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's denial of defendants' motion to dismiss the complaint on the issue of constitutional impairment of obligation of contract, and as to the certification of the suit as a class action. The court reversed and remanded the denial of defendants' motion to dismiss on the issues of plaintiffs' § 1983 action and breach of fiduciary duty claim.

Faulkenbury v. Teachers' & State Employees' Retirement System


No. 9110SC1023


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


108 N.C. App. 357; 424 S.E.2d 420; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 100


May 14, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 5, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants from orders denying defendants' motions to dismiss the complaint and from order certifying the action as a class action. These orders were entered 28 June 1991 in Wake County Superior Court by Judge Narley L. Cashwell.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed as to the trial court's denial of defendants' motion to dismiss on the issue of constitutional impairment of obligation of contract and as to the certification of the lawsuit as a class claim. Reversed and remanded to the Superior Court with instructions to enter motions to dismiss on the issues of: Plaintiffs' 42 U.S.C. § 1983 action and plaintiffs' breach of fiduciary duty claim.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff teachers sought declaratory relief against defendants, the State, the retirement system, and officials (retirement system), claiming that an amendment to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 135-5 was unconstitutional. The retirement system filed a motion to dismiss, and the teachers sought certification as a class. The Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) denied the motion to dismiss and granted certification. The retirement system appealed.


OVERVIEW: The teachers filed an action against the retirement system after N.C. Gen. Stat. § 135-5, which calculated retirement benefits, was amended. The teachers alleged that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 135-5(d4) violated their rights under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, constituted an unconstitutional impairment of a contract obligation pursuant to U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, and amounted to a breach of fiduciary duty. The trial court granted the teachers' motion for certification of a class, but denied the retirement system's motion to dismiss. The retirement system appealed. The court affirmed with respect to the claim for impairment of a contract obligation, holding that a contractual obligation existed and the legislature's actions impaired that obligation. The court approved the certification of the class because each member enjoyed a common vested contractual right. However, the court reversed with respect to the 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 claim, finding that the retirement system was not a "person" for purposes of § 1983 and the statute of limitations under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1-52 barred the teachers' claim for injunctive relief. The court found that the teachers failed to assert a breach of fiduciary duty.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the certification of the class and the denial of the retirement system's motion to dismiss the claim for unconstitutional impairment of a contract obligation, but reversed and remanded the judgment regarding the claims for deprivation of constitutional rights and breach of fiduciary duty, directing that a motion to dismiss be entered on those claims.

In re Appeal of Philip Morris U.S.A.


No. 9110PTC762


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


108 N.C. App. 514; 424 S.E.2d 222; 1993 N.C. App. LEXIS 91


August 26, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
January 5, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by Cabarrus County from a Final Decision of the Property Tax Commission entered 24 May 1991. Appeal and cross-appeal by Philip Morris U.S.A. from the same Final Decision.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant county and plaintiff taxpayer sought review of a final decision of the North Carolina Property Tax Commission (commission), which held that a contract between the county and a private auditing firm that granted the firm a contingency fee for its discovery of taxes owed to the county was void as against public policy.


OVERVIEW: The county and private firm entered into a contract under which the firm received a fee consisting of a percentage of unsatisfied tax obligations discovered by the firm. After an audit conducted by the firm, the assessor determined that the taxpayer owed substantial taxes to the county. The taxpayer appealed the determination to a county review board and the commission, contending that the firm had no authority to conduct the audit because the contract between the county and firm was void as against public policy. The commission entered a final decision holding that the contract and tax discovery were void. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision. The court held that (1) close scrutiny of a contingency fee contract was required when there was any question as to its reasonableness; (2) the contingency fee contract violated the principles of equality and uniformity in taxation required by N.C. Const. art. V, § 2 because fairness and impartiality were threatened when a private organization had a financial stake in the amount of tax collected as a result of assessments it recommended; and (3) there was no violation of the taxpayer's due process and equal protection rights.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the commission's decision holding that a contract between county and firm that granted the firm a contingency fee for its discovery of taxes owed to the county was void as against public policy.

 

985 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Nobles


No. 913SC627


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


107 N.C. App. 627; 422 S.E.2d 78; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 798


February 18, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
October 20, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by the State from order entered 14 February 1991 by Judge William C. Griffin, Jr., in Pitt County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed an order from the Pitt County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted defendant's motion to dismiss the charge of refusing to allow an inspection of a licensed fish dealership pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 113-136(k) (Cum. Supp. 1991) on the ground that § 113-136(k) allowed unreasonable warrantless searches and seizures in violation of the Fourth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The court found that the business operated by defendant, which was a wholesale fish dealership, was closely regulated in the state and, thus, defendant had a low expectation of privacy for purposes of determining whether a Fourth Amendment violation occurred. The statute and its surrounding regulatory scheme put defendant on notice concerning the inevitability of inspections. The court also concluded that the maintenance, preservation, protection, and development of the state's marine resources served substantial state interests. Additionally, the court found that the interests were furthered by the regulatory scheme that included warrantless searches. Finally, the court concluded that the statute was structured to limit the discretion of the inspecting officers and, thus, provided a constitutionally adequate substitute for a warrant. The court determined that the statute was limited in scope and limited as to the places that could be searched. The court held that the statute's failure to specify time limits was not constitutionally fatal. Therefore, the court reversed the judgment and held that the trial court erred in finding § 113-136(k) unconstitutional on its face.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order striking down as unconstitutional the statute under which defendant was charged.

State ex rel. Thornburg v. Lot & Bldgs.


No. 9121SC742


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


107 N.C. App. 559; 421 S.E.2d 374; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 757


August 25, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
October 6, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant Edward Bowman from judgment entered 14 March 1991 in Forsyth County Superior Court by Judge James A. Beaty, Jr. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of forfeiture pertaining to certain of his property which was entered by the Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina) pursuant to the provisions of the North Carolina RICO forfeiture statute.


OVERVIEW: In this forfeiture proceeding, defendant challenged the trial court's order of forfeiture pertaining to certain of his property. Defendant contended that the trial court lacked subject matter jurisdiction over the case and that RICO did not apply to him. The court affirmed the trial court's order. The trial court properly determined that it had jurisdiction to adjudicate the state's civil forfeiture claim. Subject matter jurisdiction over civil cases was statutorily conferred on the superior court under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 7A-240. Also, for purposes of RICO, the gambling offenses for which defendant was convicted constituted "racketeering activity" under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 75D-3(c)(2). There was ample evidence to show that defendant's property was used in the course of a racketeering activity as required for forfeiture of the property under RICO.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order of forfeiture pertaining to certain of defendant's property.

 

987 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


In re Belk


No. 9126DC517


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


107 N.C. App. 448; 420 S.E.2d 682; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 732; 20 Media L. Rep. 1733


March 18, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
September 15, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by intervenor/petitioner from order entered 20 March 1991 in Mecklenburg County District Court by Judge Stanley Brown.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant television station sought review of an order from the Mecklenburg County District Court (North Carolina), which determined that the involuntary commitment proceedings of respondent individual should remain closed to the public. The station argued that N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 122C-251 to 271 (1989), which govern such proceedings, were unconstitutional because they violated the right of public access to the courts.


OVERVIEW: The individual had been kept in a wire cage by a church after his family sought help in controlling him. The station investigated and reported the story. Thereafter, the individual was held at the county mental health center. A hearing was scheduled to determine whether he should be committed to a state hospital. The station, with others, was allowed to intervene for the purpose of determining whether the hearing and his medical records should be open to the public. Upon review, the court affirmed. It found that the U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV did not create a presumption that courts were open to the public in involuntary civil commitment proceedings, especially because the purpose of such proceedings was to protect the individual. It found N.C. Const. art. I, § 18 did not create a constitutional right on behalf of the public to attend civil commitment proceedings. Further, as N.C. Gen. Stat. § 122C specifically addressed the procedure for civil commitment hearings, its provisions controlled over the general public records provisions of N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 109, 190, and 191. The trial court had the right to deny access to those whose interests were purely self-serving.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court that determined the involuntary commitment proceedings of the individual were not open to the public.

Household Fin. Corp. v. Ellis


No. 9121DC493


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


107 N.C. App. 262; 419 S.E.2d 592; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 683


March 16, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
August 18, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from order entered 28 February 1991 by Judge Margaret L. Sharpe in Forsyth County District Court.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant debtor sought review of the order of the Forsyth County District Court (North Carolina), which permitted appellee creditor to execute on multiple judgments without multiple notices to designate exemptions.


OVERVIEW: The debtor was notified of an execution and failed to respond within the time designated by statute. Subsequent executions were issued without additional notice to the debtor. On appeal, the court reversed. The court determined that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1C-1603(a)(4) required that no execution be issued until a notice to designate exemptions was served and any waiver applied only to the particular execution issued. The court determined that the legislature did not intend to require a debtor to forever waive its rights for failure to respond to a single notice. The court determined that exemptions could be claimed until the moment the money from the sale was applied to the debt to be paid and therefore the provisions of N. C. Gen. Stat. § 1C-1601 and § 1C-1603 were unconstitutional because they limited the claiming of constitutional exemptions to 20 days after notice to designate was served.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's decision and ordered that notice must be issued for each execution.

State v. Davis


No. 914SC606


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


106 N.C. App. 596; 418 S.E.2d 263; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 547


February 19, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
July 7, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from judgment entered 15 October 1990 by Judge Henry L. Stevens, III, in Sampson County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction in the Sampson County Superior Court (North Carolina) for one count of taking indecent liberties with a minor and one count of first degree rape.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with multiple offenses involving an alleged kidnapping and attack of a young boy and girl. After the first trial, he was acquitted of the kidnapping count as to the boy. The jury was unable to agree as to the counts relating to the girl. After a second trial, defendant was convicted of the two offenses and acquitted on the kidnapping count. Defendant appealed his convictions. Double jeopardy did not require that the charges for which he was tried at the second trial be dismissed because the offenses of attempted first degree rape and taking indecent liberties with a minor did not require proof of the same elements as were necessary in the kidnapping charges. In addition, the retrial following the hung jury with regard to the charges relating to the girl did not violate the Double Jeopardy Clause. The eyewitness testimony of the boy was admissible under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 8C-1, N.C. R. Evid. 403. It was error to admit the testimony regarding the girl's post traumatic stress disorder without a limiting instruction. However, the error was not reversible because of strong and convincing eyewitness evidence that corroborated the testimony of the girl.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions of defendant for taking indecent liberties with a minor and first degree rape.

County of Lancaster v. Mecklenburg County


No. 9126SC206


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


106 N.C. App. 646; 417 S.E.2d 827; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 550


December 3, 1991, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
July 7, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendants from judgment dated 17 January 1991, and orders dated 17 January 1991 and 18 January 1991 by Judge Shirley L. Fulton in Mecklenburg County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed a decision of the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina), which found in favor of plaintiff county officials on their action to review a decision by the county board of adjustment.


OVERVIEW: On appeal the defendants contended that the trial court erred by failing to recognize that (1) a fundamental difference exists between zoning permits and special use or conditional use permits; (2) the county board of commissioners had exclusive authority to determine which uses will be permitted on a zoning district by zoning district basis; and (3) the matters presented in the complaint could be presented only in the manner prescribed by N.C. Gen. Stat. § 153A-345. The court agreed in part and reversed, holding that even if the permit issued was a special or conditional use permit, the county board of commissioners had authority to delegate their authority to the zoning administrator to issue special use zoning permits under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 153A-340. They also had the authority to allow the zoning administrator to issue permits for "uses by right subject to special requirements."


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of the county officials was reversed and remanded.

Semones v. Southern Bell Tel. & Tel. Co.


No. 9126SC478


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


106 N.C. App. 334; 416 S.E.2d 909; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 489


March 12, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
June 2, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from order entered 26 February 1991 in Mecklenburg County Superior Court by Judge Loto Greenlee Caviness.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff customer appealed from an order of the Mecklenburg County Superior Court (North Carolina), which granted defendant telephone company's motion for summary judgment on both of the customer's claims for malicious prosecution and abuse of process.


OVERVIEW: The telephone company threatened the customer with legal action pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 14-107 for a dishonored check he wrote as president of a business. The telephone company knew an involuntary bankruptcy petition had been filed against the business and filed a proof of claim in bankruptcy court. It then procured a warrant for the customer's arrest. The district attorney later dismissed the charges. On appeal, the court reversed the summary judgment on the customer's malicious prosecution claim. The check's return due to insufficient funds was not prima facie evidence that the customer knew there were insufficient funds to pay it. Two elements of malicious prosecution were undisputed. The court found that the telephone company lacked probable cause to initiate the worthless check proceeding absent evidence to believe that the customer knew there were insufficient funds. The telephone company lacked probable cause to initiate the prosecution and thus was not entitled to summary judgment on that claim. However, summary judgment on the abuse of process claim was affirmed as no meritorious evidence was presented of an improper act by the telephone company.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of summary judgment against the customer on his claim for malicious prosecution, and affirmed the grant of summary judgment against the customer on his abuse of process claim. The case was remanded for further proceedings.

Dunn v. Pate


No. 9112SC324


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


106 N.C. App. 56; 415 S.E.2d 102; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 348


January 15, 1992, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
April 7, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiffs from an order granting defendants' motion for summary judgment entered 31 December 1990 by Judge D.B. Herring, Jr.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individuals challenged the judgment of the trial court (North Carolina), which granted summary judgment to defendant individuals with respect to an action on a deed, after holding that N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 52-6, 47-39 were unconstitutional as a form of gender-based discrimination.


OVERVIEW: On remand from the court's earlier reversal of plaintiffs' deed action, the trial court held that N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 52-6, 47-39 was violative of the Equal Protection Clause of the 14th Amendment to the United States Constitution, the Due Process Clause of the 14th Amendment to the United States Constitution and N.C. Const. art. 1, § 19. On appeal, plaintiffs argued that the constitutionality of the statutes had previously been determined by the SUPREME COURT OF NORTH CAROLINA and the trial court was bound to follow the established precedent. The court reversed the judgment and held that the trial court erred in concluding the statutes were unconstitutional because the supreme court had upheld the constitutionality of the statutes and had stated that the validity of the statutes as a constitutional exercise of legislative power and its application to deeds could not be further questioned. The court noted that the courts of North Carolina followed the judicial policy of stare decisis. The doctrine was particularly applicable when property rights, especially rights in real property, were concerned and when the rights had become vested in reliance on the precedents.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment and remanded with direction that summary judgment be entered in favor of plaintiffs.

 

993 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Sherrod v. North Carolina Dep't of Human Resources


No. 9110SC241


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


105 N.C. App. 526; 414 S.E.2d 50; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 248


December 4, 1991, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
March 3, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Wake County. No. 90-CVS-187. Appeal by petitioner from judgment entered 25 January 1991 in Wake County Superior Court by Judge Henry V. Barnette, Jr. 



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public employee appealed from the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which affirmed the determination of North Carolina Department Of Human Resources that the department lacked subject matter jurisdiction in the public employee's grievance related to her discharge.


OVERVIEW: Following the employee's discharge, she filed a grievance. Subsequently, the department ruled that it no longer had jurisdiction because the employee had abandoned her grievance by failing to timely file during the third step of the process. The trial court affirmed the department's determination. The court affirmed the judgment and held that: (1) the employee's due process rights were fully protected because the grievance process afforded her the opportunity for a full and fair hearing, only requiring that she follow procedural rules; (2) the employee received notice of the reason for her termination, that being that certain property, identified as belonging to the employer, had been found in her residence pursuant to the execution of a search warrant; (3) the time provision was clear with regard to the distinction between working and calendar days, as were the letters the employee received containing information on how she was to appeal to the next step; and (4) the decision to enforce the deadline was not arbitrary and capricious because she was informed at each level of the process and was also given information on how to obtain procedural guidance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's affirmation of the department's ruling that it no longer had subject matter jurisdiction.

 

994 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Newsome v. North Carolina State Bd. of Elections


No. 9110SC25


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


105 N.C. App. 499; 415 S.E.2d 201; 1992 N.C. App. LEXIS 250


October 14, 1991, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
March 3, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected March 10, 1992. Second Correction April 15, 1992. Third Correction May 21, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by plaintiffs from judgment entered 29 October 1990 in Wake County Superior Court by Judge George R. Greene. Wake County. No. 90 CV 09699.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina) entered judgment against plaintiffs, incumbent aldermen, in their action against defendants, state board of election and board members, and intervening defendants, incumbent alderman and candidates. Plaintiffs sought to prevent certification of an election. Plaintiffs appealed the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The general assembly created a new town and allowed for an election for the qualified voters of the area to decide whether or not to incorporate. The election was not held on the scheduled date because it was not precleared by the United States Attorney General as required by 42 U.S.C.S. § 1973c, but it was rescheduled and incorporation was approved. Defendants then ordered a special election for the election of a governing body. Plaintiffs argued that defendants did not have the authority to call a special election. The court affirmed the judgment against plaintiffs. The court rejected plaintiff's argument that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 163-22.2 (1991) provided for the rescheduling of an election only when the delay occurred because the justice department had interposed an objection to the election procedures. The statute was a remedial statute that could be construed liberally. The effect of the failure to hold the first election because it had not been precleared was the same as if an objection had been interposed. That effect was sufficient to authorize defendant to take remedial action, such as holding a special election.


OUTCOME: The judgment against plaintiffs on behalf of defendants and intervening defendants, which certified the election results, was affirmed.

 

995 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


State v. Allen


No. 902SC14


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


102 N.C. App. 598; 403 S.E.2d 907; 1991 N.C. App. LEXIS 468


September 25, 1990, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
May 7, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by defendant from Judgment of Judge Thomas S. Watts entered 17 August 1989 in Washington County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: No error.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Washington County Superior Court (North Carolina), which convicted her of knowingly maintaining a building used for the keeping of marijuana in violation of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 90-108(a)(7).


OVERVIEW: The police came to defendant's residence and told her they had a warrant for her arrest and to search the residence and the outbuilding, and that they would get her husband from work and return him to the residence. The police found marijuana-growing equipment and plants in the outbuilding. After they read defendant her rights, she admitted that she knew what her husband was doing and had taken money for payment of the electric bill for the outbuilding. She filed a motion to suppress the evidence found during the search and her statement. The trial court found that the search of the residence was improper and that there was no probable cause for defendant's arrest. The court allowed, however, the statement and evidence from the outbuilding. Defendant was convicted of knowingly maintaining a building used for the keeping of marijuana. On appeal, the court affirmed the conviction because it found that defendant knowingly waived her rights and gave a statement to police and that the police had not exploited the illegality of the search of the residence or defendant's arrest in order to obtain her statement. Her maintenance of the building was sufficient to support the jury's verdict.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of knowingly maintaining a building used for the keeping of marijuana.

 

996 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Weston v. Carolina Medicorp


No. 9021SC677


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


102 N.C. App. 370; 402 S.E.2d 653; 1991 N.C. App. LEXIS 444


December 14, 1990, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
April 2, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from judgment filed 22 November 1989 in Forsyth County Superior Court by Judge W. Douglas Albright.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff physician sought review of the judgment of the Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina) which denied his petition for injunctive relief against defendants, non-profit corporation and affiliated hospital (hospital), after the hospital terminated the physician's staff privileges, adopting the hospital's proposed findings of fact and conclusions of law.


OVERVIEW: The hospital revoked the physician's staff privileges. The trial court denied injunctive relief, concluding that termination was not state action, that he had not contract with the hospital, and that the hospital's actions were not arbitrary, capricious, or illegal. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendants. The court held that the physician had no due process claim because the hospital's termination of his staff privileges did not constitute state action as neither the state nor any local government created any legal framework governing the challenged conduct, delegated authority to the hospital or accepted any benefits from unconstitutional behavior, and health care did not involved the exercise by a private entity of powers traditionally reserved to the state. The hospital's action in disciplining the physician for failure to comply with its HIV infection policy was not wrongful, arbitrary or capricious because the policy was consistent with N.C. Gen. Stat. § 130A-143. The physician had no 42 U.S.C.S. § 1981 claim because had no contract with the hospital. Finally, the trial court's findings and conclusions were supported by competent evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the hospital denying the injunctive relief sought by the physician to reinstate his hospital staff privileges.

Estridge v. Ford Motor Co.


No. 9026SC444


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


101 N.C. App. 716; 401 S.E.2d 85; 1991 N.C. App. LEXIS 145


December 3, 1990, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
February 19, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from an order entered 17 January 1990 by Judge Shirley L. Fulton in the Civil Session of the Superior Court of Mecklenburg County.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff purchaser appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Mecklenburg County (North Carolina) that granted defendant seller's motion to dismiss, brought pursuant to N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6) and 41(b). The trial court dismissed the purchaser's claim, which was based on the New Motor Vehicles Warranties Act, N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 20-351 to 351.10, for failing to state a cause of action upon which relief could be granted.


OVERVIEW: After the purchaser's new automobile could not be fixed, the purchaser brought a cause of action under the "lemon law." The seller's motion to dismiss was granted. The court affirmed, holding that the purchaser failed to state a claim upon which relief could be granted in that N.C. Gen. Stat. §§ 20-351 to 351.10 was not intended to be applied retroactively. The trial judge was correct in dismissing the plaintiff's cause of action under N.C. Gen. Stat. § 1A-1 and N.C. R. Civ. P. 12(b)(6), for failure to state a claim upon which relief could be granted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the trial court that dismissed the purchaser's claim brought pursuant to the lemon law, for failing to state a claim upon which relief could be granted.

State ex rel. Environmental Management Comm'n v. House of Raeford Farms, Inc.


No. 904SC283


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


101 N.C. App. 433; 400 S.E.2d 107; 1991 N.C. App. LEXIS 86


September 27, 1990, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
February 5, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiffs and defendants from judgment and order entered 1 August 1989 by Judge D. Marsh McLelland in Duplin County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part; vacated in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Environmental Management Commission (EMC) and defendant corporations entered into a consent judgment to settle ten enforcement proceedings pending against the corporations. The EMC initiated enforcement proceedings that assessed civil penalties. The corporations sought relief in the Duplin County Superior Court (North Carolina) and obtained a judgment that, among other things, set aside the civil penalties assessment.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the EMC assigned as error: (1) the trial court's exercise of jurisdiction; (2) the trial court's conclusion that the EMC's consideration of the statutory criteria was perfunctory in setting the penalty amounts; (3) the trial court's reversal of the EMC's assessment of civil penalties; and (4) the trial court's modification of the interim effluent limits in the consent judgment. The corporations assigned as error: (1) the trial court's decision not to modify the consent judgment to permanently change the effluent limitations; (2) the trial court's refusal to find that the civil penalties assessed against them were unconstitutional; (3) the trial court's conclusion that the EMC was not barred by the statute of limitations; (4) the trial court's conclusion that the they were liable for the penalty stipulated in the consent judgment; and (5) the trial court's denial of their motion for attorney fees. After review, this court concluded that the EMC's assessment of penalties was not subject to judicial review because the corporations failed to exhaust their administrative remedies and that the EMC was prejudiced by the judge's modification of the consent judgment.


OUTCOME: The superior court's judgment was vacated as to the temporary modifications of the effluent limitations in the consent judgment and as to the reversal of the civil penalty. The remaining portions of the superior court's judgment were affirmed.

 

999 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


47th Street Photo, Inc. v. Powers


No. 9010SC2


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


100 N.C. App. 746; 398 S.E.2d 52; 1990 N.C. App. LEXIS 1159


August 24, 1990, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
December 4, 1990, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal by plaintiff from Order entered 1 November 1989 by Judge Samuel T. Currin in Wake County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff out-of-state merchant appealed a judgment of the Wake County Superior Court (North Carolina), which dismissed the merchant's declaratory judgment action and petition for an injunction to enjoin defendants, the North Carolina Department of Revenue and its Secretary of Revenue, from requiring the merchant to collect and pay sales and use taxes.


OVERVIEW: The merchant argued that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-164.8(b) violated the Commerce Clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 8 and the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The merchant requested that defendants be permanently enjoined from requiring it to collect the state sales tax on out of-state orders. The trial court concluded that N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-267 barred the relief sought by the merchant and required the dismissal of its action. On appeal, the court agreed. Section 105-267, which was constitutional, required the merchant to pay the taxes under protest and then seek a refund. If the Department did not issue a refund, the merchant could then file its action against the Secretary. The merchant was required to comply with § 105-267 when it sought to challenge the constitutionality of a statute or its application to it. Thus, the trial court was without authority to issue an injunction or to consider the constitutionality of N.C. Gen. Stat. § 105-164.8(b) when the merchant had not collected and paid the sales taxes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

1000 of 1540 DOCUMENTS


Allen v. Rupard


No. 9021SC146


COURT OF APPEALS OF NORTH CAROLINA


100 N.C. App. 490; 397 S.E.2d 330; 1990 N.C. App. LEXIS 1059


September 18, 1990, Heard in the Court of Appeals  
October 30, 1990, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended. As Corrected November 20, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal by Pilot Freight Carriers and Protective Insurance Company from order entered on 29 November 1989 by Judge W. Steven Allen, Sr., in Forsyth County Superior Court.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, employer and its workers' compensation insurer, sought review of an order by the Forsyth County Superior Court (North Carolina), which determined that appellee injured employee and the employer's insurer equally shared the settlement proceeds from a third party action.


OVERVIEW: The employee was injured in a two-truck collision. The driver of the other truck tendered $ 25,000, the policy limits of his liability insurance. The employer's insurer had paid over $ 40,000 in workers' compensation payments to the employee and held a subrogation lien against any third part recovery. The employee requested the trial court to determine the distribution of the settlement proceeds pursuant to N.C. Gen. Stat. § 97-10.2(j). The employer's insurer argued that it was entitled to the entire amount under § 97-10.2(f). On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not have to follow the distribution priority schedule under § 97-10.2(f), which was binding on the industrial commission but not the trial court. The court found that the legislature intended to provide a different standard. Rejecting appellants' constitutional challenge, the court held that § 97-10.2(j) did not give the trial court unlimited discretion to determine distribution of the proceeds. Affirming the order, the court found that the trial court's distribution of proceeds, which fell short of reimbursing either the employee or the insurer, was a reasoned choice that was factually supported.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order, which equally divided the settlement proceeds from the third party tort action between the employee and the employer's workers' compensation insurer.
